TFL 6 Management Plan #10  Page 1 
1 Introduction 
 
This is the first Management Plan (MP) prepared for Tree Farm Licence (TFL) 6 to meet the 
requirements of the Tree Farm Licence Management Plan Regulation (B.C. Reg. 280/2009).  This 
regulation, enacted by the provincial government in November 2009 (with associated 
amendments to the Forest Act), includes content requirements, submission timing and public 
review requirements for TFL Management Plans.  These content requirements (in regulation) 
replace the MP content requirements listed in the tree farm licence document and reduce the 
duplication of Forest Stewardship Plan matters (objectives and strategies).  The content item of 
greatest interest is likely the timber supply analysis that will provide information to the Chief 
Forester of BC for the determination of the next Allowable Annual Cut (AAC) for TFL 6.  
 
2 Description of TFL 6 
 
TFL 6 is located on northern Vancouver Island in the vicinity of Quatsino Sound.  It extends from 
Nahwitti Lake in the north to Victoria Lake in the south, and from Winter Harbour in the west to 
Port McNeill in the east (refer to Figure 1). 
 
TFL 6 currently covers over 171,000 ha, approximately six-sevenths of which is productive forest 
land.  The major tree species include western hemlock, western red cedar and balsam (amabilis 
fir), with lesser amounts of Sitka spruce, Douglas-fir, yellow cedar, alder and shore pine.  The 
forests of TFL 6 predominantly lie within the wetter maritime Coastal Western Hemlock 
biogeoclimatic zone.  Annual precipitation levels reach 3,000 to 5,000 mm.  The climate is 
characterized by mild wet winters with daily mean minimum temperatures from December to 
February in the 0oC to 2oC range.  Summers are generally cool and moist with daily mean 
maximum temperatures during July and August only in the 18oC to 20oC range.  However, local 
climates within the TFL can be significantly different due to topographic influences and the 
movement of low cloud and fog from offshore onto northern Vancouver Island.  
 
The topography of TFL 6 is varied with mountainous steep formations dominating the landscape 
of the central and inland portions and rolling gentle terrain characterizing the eastern and western 
sections.  Numerous rivers and streams drain the licence area with several supporting significant 
anadromous and resident fish populations.  Large animals, notably Columbia black -tailed deer, 
cougars and black bears are common throughout the licence area.  Numerous other mammals, 
amphibians and birds can also be found.  
 
There are seven communities within or adjacent to the licence area.  These are Port McNeill, Port 
Hardy, Port Alice, Holberg, Winter Harbour, Coal Harbour and Quatsino.  The livelihood of these 
communities and their stability depends mostly on the economic activities generated on the North 
Island.  Forest management and forest product manufacturing, along with the associated support 
services, are the major employment activities in this region.  Other significant economic activities 
include aquaculture, commercial and recreational fishing and tourism.

3 TFL 6 Licence Holder History 
 
Forest Management Licence (FML) No. 6 (Quatsino) was originally awarded in 1950.  FMLs were 
later renamed Tree Farm Licences (TFLs).  The licence holder has changed over time with 
successive corporate name changes, acquisitions and mergers (see Table 1). 
 
Table 1 - TFL 6 Licence Holders 
Licence 
Date listed 
company became 
licence holder Licence Holder Description 
FML 6 October 26, 1950 British Columbia Pulp and 
Paper Company Limited Original FML 
FML 6 April 25, 1951 Alaska Pine and Cellulose 
Limited Company name change 
TFL 6 May 22, 1958 Alaska Pine and Cellulose 
Limited FMLs become TFLs 
TFL 6 October 20, 1959 Rayonier Canada Limited Company name change 
TFL 6 May 25, 1961 Rayonier B.C. Limited Assignment to subsidiary 
TFL 6 September 1, 1961 Rayonier Canada (B.C.) 
Limited Company name change 
TFL 6 October 31, 1980 Western Forest Products 
Limited Corporate purchase 
TFL 6 July 13, 2004 Western Forest Products Inc. Corporate purchase and 
name change 
 
 
4 TFL 6 Consolidations and Subdivisions 
 
In October 1998, Western Forest Products Limited consolidated its TFLs 6, 24 and 25.  As a 
result, Block 4 of TFL 25 was eliminated and amalgamated into TFL 6 as Block 2 .  This block 
comprises the watersheds of Queen Charlotte Strait between Fort Rupert and Port McNeil l, and 
portions of the Quatsino Sound watershed (see Figure 1 and Table 2). 
 
Table 2 - TFL 6 Consolidations and Subdivisions 
Date Boundary Change 
October 1, 1998 Consolidation of TFL 25 Block 4 into TFL 6 as Block 2 
 
 
5 Major TFL 6 Boundary Changes 
 
Table 3 lists changes to the TFL area involving over 200 hectares and the date of those changes.  
This list may omit some boundary changes for which records have been lost or could not be 
found when preparing this document.  There have been numerous minor (< 200 ha) area 
revisions since 1950 to add area to the TFL and to accommodate other land uses such as gravel 
pits, electrical distribution infrastructure, and town sites.

7 Public Review Strategy Summary 
 
Opportunity to review and provide comments on the TFL 6 Draft Management Plan (MP) #10 was based on a 
strategy approved by the Regional Executive Director on March 2, 2010.  The first phase was public review and 
First Nations’ information-sharing of a draft timber supply analysis information package (IP).  The second phase 
was public review and First Nations’ information-sharing of a draft MP that included the accepted IP and the 
timber supply analysis (TSA) results.   
 
The public review, including information-sharing with First Nations, of MP #5 began in October 2010.  On or 
about October 20, 2010 copies of the draft IP were provided to the following provincial government agencies: 
 
Ministry of Forests, Lands and Natural Resource 
Operations1 (MoFLNRO) - Forest Analysis and 
Inventory Branch (FAIB) 
MoFLNRO – Coast Region 
MoFLNRO – North Island Central – Coast Resource 
District2 (NICCRD) 
Ministry of Environment (MoE) – Vancouver Island 
Region (Ecosystems Section) 
 
Maps associated with the MP were sent to each MoFLNRO office.  MoE was sent a CD containing the 
documents and the maps.  WFP offered to print the maps for MoE if requested to do so (this was not 
requested). 
 
On or about October 20, 2010 copies of the draft IP (on CD) were provided to the following First Nations and 
First Nation organizations: 
 
Nanwakolas Council (for Kwakiutl and 
Mamalilikulla Qwe’qwa’sot’em First Nations) Namgis First Nation 
Quatsino First Nation Tlatlasikwala First Nation 
 
The CD contained the draft IP document and the associated maps.  WFP offered to print the maps for First 
Nations if requested to do so (this was not requested).  Follow-up letters were sent to each of the above First 
Nations and First Nation organizations on November 16, 2010 reminding them of the opportunity to provide 
comments on the draft IP. 
 
Notification letters were sent to interested stakeholders (based on a contact list that included water licence 
holders, trappers, guide outfitters, local governments, and WFP’s CSA advisory group).  Ads were run in the 
North Island Gazette newspaper on October 21st and October 28th, 2010.  The ads stated that the draft IP was 
available for review and comment from October 21, 2010 until December 20, 2010 at the following locations: 
 
 WFP Port McNeill office 
 WFP Holberg office

of Natural Resource Operations.  Some agency contacts worked for this Ministry during its short existence.

TFL 6 –Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 1 
1 INTRODUCTION 
This Information Package (IP) provides a summary of data, assumptions, and modelling procedures to be 
used in the Timber Supply Analysis (TSA) for Western Forest Products‟ (WFP) Tree Farm Licence (TFL) 
6 Management Plan (MP) #10.   
The timber supply analysis will be conducted using Remsoft‟s Spatial Planning System 
(www.remsoft.com).  Woodstock is the aspatial component of the suite and addresses the majority of the 
model objectives and constraints.  Woodstock performs a similar function as the Ministry of Forests and 
Range‟s FSSIM model whereby management zones and constraints are defined, yield curves 
incorporated and applied to an aggregated area file.  The primary difference between Woodstock and 
FSSIM is that Woodstock is capable of using either optimization or sequential simulation in developing a 
harvest forecast.   
Stanley, the spatial component of the suite, applies the Woodstock harvest forecast to specific polygons 
on the land base.  Stanley aggregates individual polygons into suitable harvest units (blocks) based on 
specified minimum, maximum and target block sizes.  The model also enforce s green-up and adjacency 
requirements as it schedules the harvest spatially.   
For this analysis, optimization will be used in Woodstock to develop the base case harvest schedule.  The 
optimization will be subject to a number of harvest constraints including the requirement to produce a 
long-term sustainable harvest forecast.  Stanley will not be used for the analysis. 
WFP will complete the timber supply analysis to estimate timber harvest over a 250-year planning horizon 
(in five-year planning periods) based on the current harvestable land base, existing old forest timber 
volumes, and regenerating forest growth rates.  The harvest forecast will project the timber supply impacts 
of current environmental protection and management practices including operational requirements of the 
Forest and Range Practices Act (FRPA), approved Forest Stewardship Plan (FSP), orders, other 
regulations and guidelines with significance to timber supply.  Sensitivity analyses will be used to 
investigate the expected impacts of different management scenarios, and to examine the relative 
importance of variations in assumptions.  These may include the removal of area from the timber 
harvesting land base (THLB), imposing forest-cover harvest constraints, or changes in growth & yield 
(G&Y) estimates.   
The timber supply forecast will attempt to achieve the long-term harvest potential, and minimize the rate of 
change during the transition from the current level of harvest to the mid- and long-term sustainable levels.

2 PROCESS 
This Information Package is being submitted for acceptance by the Timber Supply Forester at Forest 
Analysis and Inventory Branch of the Ministry of Forests, Mines and Lands (MFML – formerly Ministry of 
Forests and Range (MoFR)). This version addresses comments and questions raised during the review 
process associated with the draft Information package (Version 1 – October 2010). 
Version 1 of this Information Package was submitted for review to the MFML Timber Supply Forester at 
Forest Analysis and Inventory Branch and Ministry of Natural Resource Operations (MNRO – formerly 
MoFR) staff in the North Island – Central Coast Resource District (NICCRD – formerly NICC Forest 
District).  It was also made available for review by stakeholders, the general public and potentially affected 
First Nations.   
This revised and accepted Information Package will guide the timber supply analysis.  Once completed, 
the timber supply analysis will be made available for review as a component of a draft Management Plan 
#10 for TFL 6.  The final accepted timber supply analysis will be considered by the Chief Forester in 
determining the new Allowable Annual Cut (AAC) for TFL 6.

TFL 6 –Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 4 
3 TIMBER SUPPLY FORECASTS/OPTIONS/SENSITIVITY ANALYSES 
This section describes the landbase and management scenarios to be included in the timber supply 
analysis. The details, assumptions, and sensitivities of each are also described. 
3.1 Current Management Option 
The current management option (or the “Base Case”) represents the present operational requirements 
and management practices on the TFL.  The forecast of current management incorporates existing land 
use designations, including Resource Management Zones1, and current regulations and guidelines 
including the Forest and Range Practices Act and the approved Forest Stewardship Plan.  This option is 
used as the basis for analysing various timber supply projections. 
Current management on TFL 6 includes: 
 The operable landbase of forested area accessible using conventional and non-conventional 
(helicopter) harvesting methods. 
 Restricted performance in the non-conventional landbase (see Section 10.3.3). 
 Exclusion of uneconomic forest stands. 
 Harvesting of both mature and immature stands. 
 Biodiversity and Landscape Units – Established Old Growth Management Areas (OGMAs) in the 
San Josef and Marble landscape units are removed from the THLB.  Also removed are draft 
OGMAs in the Holberg, Keogh, Mahatta, and Neroutsos landscape units.  Mature seral targets are 
incorporated for the two Special Management Zones within TFL 6. 
 Established Ungulate Winter Ranges (UWRs) and Wildlife Habitat Areas are removed from the 
THLB.  
 Riparian management based on the FSP results/strategies and a review of stand-level retention 
within harvested cutblocks between 1997 and 2008. 
 Wildlife Tree and other stand-level retention within the THLB are accounted for by a percentage 
area reduction. 
 Visual quality objectives (VQOs) modelled based on proposed VQOs resulting from the recently 
accepted (May 2010) Visual Landscape Inventory for TFL 6 with upper range disturbance 
assumed.  The North Island – Central Coast Resource District (NICCRD) used these proposed 
VQOs in undertaking the required steps for establishing VQOs as legal objectives through the 
Government Actions Regulation (GAR).  The established VQOs differ slightly from those that will 
be used in the analysis.  The differences will be discussed in the timber supply analysis. 
 Green-up heights for cutblock adjacency are assigned based on Resource Management Zones 
established in the Vancouver Island Higher Level Plan.  Special and General zones have a 3m 
green-up requirement while Enhanced zones have a 1.3m green-up height.  
 Silviculture to meet free growing requirements is carried out on all regenerated stands.  The 
majority of harvested areas are planted. 
 Known tree improvement gains will be applied to existing stands ≤ 10 years old and future 
regenerated stands. 
                                                      
 
1 Vancouver Island land Use Plan (VILUP) Resource Management Zones and Resource Management Zone objectives approved by 
Government in December 2000.     

--- page 27 ---
     February 2011 
 
 
TFL 6 –Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 5 
 Minimum harvest age varies by harvesting system and the minimum harvestable volume is 350m3 
per hectare.  Both minimum age and minimum volume requirements must be met before a stand 
can be harvested.  
 A relatively small area of deciduous leading stands is excluded from the THLB while minor 
deciduous volumes in mixed stands contribute to the analysis.

of uncertainty in several key attributes.  These include the removal of operable areas from the THLB, 
imposing forest-cover harvest constraints, or changes in growth & yield estimates.

3.3 Alternate Harvest Flow 
The harvest level in the current management option will adjust over 10-year intervals (to coincide with the 
term of AAC determinations) in the first part of the run towards the estimated Long Term Harvest Level 
(LTHL).  The results of the base case will determine potential alternate harvest flows.  One option will be 
to investigate the mid-term timber supply impacts of continuing the initial harvest for 2 or more ten-year 
intervals. 
During preparation of the timber supply analysis, the need for further sensitivity analyses or harvest flows 
may become apparent. If warranted, additional analyses will be included in the final timber supply analysis 
for consideration by the Chief Forester.

     February 2011 
 
 
TFL 6 –Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 6 
4 HARVEST MODEL 
The TFL 6 timber supply analysis including harvest level and forest inventory projections will be developed 
using Remsoft‟s Spatial Planning System (www.remsoft.com).  
Woodstock is a pseudo-spatial timber supply model that projects harvesting activities across a land base 
over a specific period of time.  These models are referred to as pseudo-spatial because the data used to 
create the model has spatial components to it; however, the harvest schedules produced by these models 
are not spatially explicit.  It is possible to bring spatial context into this type of model by applying 
constraints to spatial attributes of the land base such as landscape units or watersheds; however harvest 
schedules produced using these types of models report the timing of the harvest of different types of 
stands as opposed to specific polygons harvested in each period.  For these reasons it is not possible to 
explicitly model spatial management objectives such as cutblock size, adjacency and green-up 
requirements, or patch size targets using this type of model. 
Woodstock uses optimization to establish a harvest schedule that incorporates objectives such as visual 
quality, biodiversity, wildlife habitat with the objective of timber harvest.  In Woodstock, harvest volume is 
maximized subject to the maintenance of other values on the land base. 

--- page 29 ---
     February 2011 
 
 
TFL 6 –Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 7 
5 FOREST COVER INVENTORY 
5.1 Vegetation Resources Inventory 
A Vegetation Resource Inventory (VRI) project for TFL 6 was initiated in 1999.  Phase I (forest cover 
polygon boundaries delineated and attributes estimated using aerial photography) was completed in 2000 
using 1:15,000 scale colour imagery taken in 1995.  Phase II (ground sampling) and the Net Volume 
Adjustment Factor (NVAF) sampling was carried out in 2001.  The last component, the statistical 
adjustment, was completed and reported on by Timberline Natural Resource Group (now Teco Natural 
Resource Group) in 2009.  The results have been reviewed by the MFML, Forest Analysis & Inventory 
Branch and approved for use in the TFL 6 MP #10 timber supply analysis. 
The VRI project was funded by Forest Renewal BC (FRBC) and Forest Investment Account (FIA).

6 DESCRIPTION OF LANDBASE 
This section describes the TFL 6 landbase and the methods used to determine the portion of the landbase 
that contributes to timber harvesting (THLB).  Some portions of the productive landbase, while not 
contributing to harvest, are crucial in meeting the demands for non-timber resource sustainability.  Areas 
within all tables in this section may not sum due to rounding to the nearest hectare. 
6.1 AAC and Landbase Changes and Allocations 
At the time of the TFL 6 MP #9 AAC Determination in 2001 the TFL area was estimated as 198,113 ha 
and the AAC was determined as 1,460,000 m3, of which 13,242 m3 was attributed to BCTS and the 
remaining 1,446,758 m3 was allocated to WFP. 
In 2003, the provincial government enacted the Forest Revitalization Act.  This legislation reallocated 20% 
of the AAC of major licensees such as WFP to others such as BC Timber Sales, First Nations and small 
tenures such as Community Forests and Woodlots.  For TFL 6 the effect was the reallocation of 86,000 
m3 of AAC from WFP to others: 69,422 m 3 to BCTS (for a new total of 82,664 m3); 11,578 m3 to First 
Nations; and 5,000 m3 to a Community Forest.  WFP‟s AAC was reduced by 81,000 m3 as of the end of 
2004 and by a further 5,000 m3 as of the end of 2005.  Areas have been deleted from TFL 6 for the total 
BCTS volume and the Community Forest volume.  Refer to Table 3 for a summary of changes in area and 
AAC since the MP #9 analysis. 
 
Table 3 - Changes in TFL 6 Area and AAC Allocations since MP #9 AAC Determination

industrial park located in the vicinity of the community of Port Alice.

6.1.1.2 Addition to Kingcome Timber Supply Area 
Also in July 2004, the TFL 6 boundary was adjusted via Instrument No. 90 in the vicinity of Mahatta River

In March 2002, 30 ha of crown land was deleted from TFL 6 via Instrument No. 84 to expand the Cluxewe 
Saltmarsh Reserve located near the mouth of the Cluxewe River. 
6.1.1.4 Marble River Park and Misty Lake Ecological Reserve  
In May 2000, WFP and the provincial government entered into a land sale agreement.  As a result, in June 
2001 Instrument No. 88 was executed - deleting 41 ha of private land from TFL 6 in the vicinity of the 
Marble River Park and Varney Bay and deleting 14 ha of private land in the vicinity of Misty Lake. 
6.1.2 Areas removed from TFL 6 
Since the MP #9 AAC determination, the total area within TFL 6 has been reduced due to removals: 
6.1.2.1 Private land 
An area of 14,027 ha was removed in January 2007 (Instrument No. 94).  The TFL 6 AAC was reduced by 
116,800 m3 to 1,343,200 m3. 
6.1.2.2 BCTS Area 
In July 2009, Instrument No. 97 deleted 11,339 ha from TFL 6 that was subsequently added to the newly 
created Pacific Timber Supply Area.  This area was attributed 82,664 m3 of the TFL 6 AAC. 
6.1.2.3 Tri-Port Community Forest 
In a January 2010 amendment to TFL 6 (Minister Order #3(4) 7-3 under the Forest Revitalization Act), 
1,072 ha were removed from TFL 6 for creating the Tri-Port Community Forest (Port McNeill, Port Hardy, 
Port Alice).  The TFL 6 AAC attributed to this area is 5,000 m3. 
6.1.3 Boundary Adjustments 
The height-of-land tenure boundaries used to create the data set to be used in this timber supply analysis 
differ slightly from the boundaries used in MP #9 with the net effect being a 122 ha reduction in the size of 
TFL 6. 
6.1.4 Remaining First Nations AAC Allocation 
Management areas for a further 11,578 m3 of Forest Revitalization AAC allocation to First Nations remain 
to be identified within the TFL.

6.4 Existing Roads 
Existing roads are excluded from the timber harvesting land base.  This reduction is due to the 
combination of classified and unclassified roads.  Classified roads are those that are mapped as forest 
cover polygons distinctly separate from adjacent polygons; a total of 138 ha of classified roads (all 
highway) were removed.  Unclassified roads have been mapped as lineal features.  For the purposes of 
determining the total area of unclassified roads, operational staff were consulted: mainlines are assumed 
to occupy a 12 metre unproductive width, branch roads are assumed to occupy a 10 metre unproductive 
width, and spur/stub roads are assumed to occupy an 8 metre unproductive width.  As all trails and the 
majority of the landings are rehabilitated and restocked following logging, the associated area reduction is 
thought insignificant.  Table 6 summarizes the areas of existing roads in the TFL.

The area removed by roads is relatively low compared to other coastal management units.  This is mainly 
due to the geography of TFL 6 which is dominated by gentle rolling terrain.  This terrain affords longer 
average yarding distances resulting in higher development per kilometre of road constructed than most 
other coastal units.

6.6 Operability 
Operability classes have been developed for the TFL 6 landbase that reflect the harvesting system, timber 
quality, terrain stability and economic accessibility.  A terrain stability inventory was completed for Block 1 
to MoFR standards in 1995 and for Block 2 in 1992, since updated to MoFR standards in 1998.   
The reclassification of the TFL‟s operability was completed in early 1999 based on a methodology 
reflecting the elements noted above and approved by the MoFR Port McNeill Forest District in September 
1998.  The classification resulted in two categories of inoperable areas being defined.  The first category 
strictly relates to the area being physically inaccessible and thus, not available for timber harvesting (I).  
The second category uses an economic criterion to determine operability (Ocm/Ohm).  Physical 
inoperability relates to the presence of a physical barrier or terrain constraint leaving accessibility virtually 
impossible.  Classifying areas as operable with an economic constraint relates to the inability to harvest 
stands in a cost-effective manner given the low market value of the timber at the time of the classification.  
Two classes are recognized in this analysis:  (1) Ocm – economically marginal areas accessible by 
conventional harvesting systems; and (2) Ohm – economically marginal heli-logging areas. 
Of the net inoperable land base, 839 ha are currently classified as Ocm/Ohm and 11,600 ha are currently 
classified as I.  The area classified as inoperable and therefore deducted from the productive forest land 
base is 12,438 ha.  Table 8 summarizes the areas removed from the THLB.

Other than unstable areas identified as inoperable no additional netdowns are made for the terrain stability

document indicates that 7.6% of the THLB is mapped as Class IV (potentially unstable) and 2.3% as 
Class V (unstable).  Spatial logging history records indicate that a total of 5488 ha of Class IV and 1725 ha 
of Class V terrain has been logged in the current TFL 6 - equal to 6.1% and 1.9% of the total area logged 
respectively. 
6.7 Riparian Management Areas 
Detailed riparian features mapping is ongoing for TFL 6 through cutblock development.  Operational 
stream inventories associated with development planning have been conducted since 1988 (with the 
introduction of the Coastal Fisheries Forestry Guidelines) and various reconnaissance fish and fish habitat 
inventory projects have been completed over time.  Therefore, a combination of 1:5,000 scale 
(operational) and 1:20,000 scale (strategic) stream data was available.  There are likely additional streams 
on the landbase that have not yet been mapped.  These would tend to be smaller streams (S4 or S6, but 
could include S3 and S5 streams). 
This detailed information provided the basis for estimating riparian classes and hence reserve areas for 
watercourses.  The approach employed in preparing the data for the timber supply analysis was to utilise 
the available stream classification in the GIS to apply Riparian Management Areas (RMAs) to known 
streams based on the sum of FRPA Riparian Reserve Zone (RRZ) widths and assumed levels of retention 
within Riparian Management Zones (RMZs).  For S1-B, S2 and S3 streams it is assumed that 50% of the 
RMZ is retained. For S4 and S5 streams RMZ retention is assumed to be 25% and for S6 streams the 
assumption is that 10% of the RMZ is retained.  For lakes and wetlands the RMZ retention levels are as 
listed in Table 9.  These RMZ values are based on a review of cutblocks harvested from 1997 to 2008 and 
the retention of riparian areas along streams both within and adjacent to the cutblocks.  The RMA 
retention levels for S4, S5 and S6 streams (25%, 25%, and 10% respectively) were marginally increased 

--- page 38 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 16 
from what was indicated by the review of harvested cutblocks as an attempt to account for unmapped 
streams. 
A 40 m “reserve zone” (netdown) will be applied to the ocean shoreline within TFL 6 to account for 
management of visual quality, operability issues and the presence of numerous eagle nests within this 
shoreline area.

6.8 Ungulate Winter Ranges 
Revised Ungulate Winter Ranges (UWRs) for Columbian black-tailed deer and Roosevelt elk were 
approved in November 2004 (U-1-010) with an amendment to the order made in May 2007. The 16 UWRs 
have a total area of 1,873 ha.  Due to conflicting tenure information, 5.3 ha of UWR NAH-08 from the 
Kingcome TSA (U-1-011) falls within the boundaries of TFL 6 being used for the timber supply analysis.  
These UWRs are included in the data set for the analysis and will be excluded from the THLB.

6.9 Old Growth Management Areas 
Landscape Units (LUs) and Biodiversity Emphasis Options (BEOs) were designated through the Order 
Establishing Provincial Non-Spatial Old Growth Objectives effective June 30, 2004 (NSOG order).  This 
order is in effect until Old Growth Management Areas (OGMAs) are spatially determined through 
Landscape Unit planning.  For TFL 6 OGMAs have been established within the San Josef (January 2005) 
and the Marble (July 2010) landscape units.  Within the Marble Enhanced Forestry Zone (EFZ), VILUP 
Objective #10 was applied to reduce the total old growth retention target by one-third within the CWHvm1 
in the Marble LU subject to identifying suitable second growth for recruitment. 
Draft OGMAs in the Holberg, Neroutsos, Keogh, and Mahatta (WFP tenures only (TFL 6 and unregulated 
Timber Licences)) landscape units have been identified to meet the NSOG order.  These draft OGMAs will 
be used in the timber supply analysis but must complete a public and First Nations‟ review process before 
coming legal.   
The draft OGMAs for landscape units with a Low BEO identify enough area to meet the old seral target 
drawn down to 1/3 for the first rotation (80 years) for all biogeoclimatic (BEC) variants.  In some cases 
enough area has been identified to meet the full target.  Targets for the end of the second rotation (160 
years) and third rotation (240 years) where necessary will be addressed as forest cover constraints (see 
Section 10.2.3 for details).

6.10 Wildlife Habitat Areas 
At the time the timber supply analysis data set was put together a total of seven Wildlife Habitat Areas 
(WHAs) had been approved within the boundaries of TFL 6 (WHAs 1-089, 1-106, 1-107, 1-199, 1-200,  
1-201 and 1-202).  One WHA was established for Northern Goshawk and six WHAs were established for 
Marbled Murrelets.    The WHAs have a total area of 659 ha with minimal impact to the THLB due to, for 
the most part, being spatially coincidental with OGMAs.

It should be noted that for the purposes of the Identified Wildlife Management Strategy (IWMS) policy 
regarding timber supply impact, the THLB impact of these WHAs is determined using MP#9 data and are 
different than the impacts indicated above in Table 12.  Furthermore, the analysis will not make an 
allowance for full implementation of the IWMS timber supply impact policy (“1% budget”) as this would be 
speculation as to where the impact will be allocated within the district (IWMS impacts are tracked at the 
district level). 
 
The Notice issued under Section 7(2) of the Forest Planning and Practices Regulation for “Indicators of 
the Amount, Distribution and Attributes of Wildlife Habitat Required for the Survival of Species at Risk in 
the North Island – Central Coast Forest District” requires licensees to maintain an equal amount of 
suitable habitat for marbled murrelet as existed in the non-THLB land base at the time of the notice 
(TSR2).  For TFL 6 TSR2 is MP #9.  An analysis was done of the marbled murrelet habitat inventory 

--- page 41 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 19 
(conducted in 2005 and 2006) against the TSR2 (MP #9) THLB and non-contributing landbase for the 
current boundaries of TFL 6.  This analysis indicated that as of the date of the habitat inventory there was 
2,886 ha of habitat in the TSR2 non-contributing landbase (Amount #1 for the Section 7 notice).   
 
The legal OGMAs (Marble and San Josef LUs) captured roughly 697 ha of TSR2 THLB identified as 
marbled murrelet habitat.  These are the only two landscape units within TFL 6 with a “THLB allocation” 
for OGMAs; therefore, the habitat captured in the legal OGMAs addresses Amount #3 for the Section 7 
notice.  An earlier analysis indicated that TFL 6 contained approximately 29.6% of the TSR2 THLB for the 
NICCFD.  Applying this factor to the amount of remaining mature THLB budget for marbled murrelet for 
the district (1,322 ha) results in 391 ha of “THLB allocation” (TSR2) for marbled murrelet within TFL 6 
(Amount #4 from the Section 7 notice).  Therefore, the maximum amount of habitat assumed to be 
required to be maintained in TFL 6 is 3,974 ha (2,886 + 697 + 391).   Analyzing the MP 10 data indicates 
that there are at least 4,631 ha of marbled murrelet habitat in the MP 10 non-contributing landbase.  Since 
the model is not allowed to harvest the non-contributing landbase at least this amount of habitat will be 
maintained.  Therefore, no constraint needs to be added to the model to ensure the Section 7 notice for 
marbled murrelets is met.

assumed to address those features.  More significant features requiring special forest management are 
identified as Recreation Sites, Trails and Interpretive Forest Sites.  These features are netted out by 
applying a 10 m buffer to the features (see Table 13).

6.13 Cultural Heritage Resources 
Archaeological sites consist of the physical remains of past human activity.  Protection and management 
of impacts to archaeological sites is governed by the Heritage Conservation Act. The term cultural 
heritage resources applies to a variety of heritage resources that is defined in the Forest Act as “an object, 
a site or the location of a traditional societal practice that is of historical, cultural or archaeological 
significance to British Columbia, a community or an aboriginal people.”  Under FRPA objectives set by 

--- page 43 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 21 
government for cultural heritage resources are to conserve, or, if necessary, protect cultural heritage 
resources that are:  
(a) the focus of a traditional use by an aboriginal people that is of continuing importance to that 
people, and  
(b) not regulated under the Heritage Conservation Act.   
An archaeological overview assessment (AOA) for the former Port McNeill Forest District was completed 
in 1995 by I.R. Wilson Consultants Ltd.  The purpose of the AOA is to identify and assess archaeological 
resource potential.  Specifically the AOA provides a basis for predictions regarding archaeological site 
variability, density and distribution and provide a framework within which to judge the significance of sites. 
A Traditional Use Study (TUS) for the Quatsino First Nation‟s traditional territory was undertaken in 1996 
and maintained to present day.  The Galgalis Traditional Use Study was compiled for the Kwakiutl 
Territorial Fisheries Commission in 1998.  This study is a collection of input from a number of the 
Kwakwaka‟wakw First Nations on Northern Vancouver Island, the south central coast of BC and the 
islands in between.  Last year, WFP was able to secure FIA funding to work with the Kwakiutl First Nation 
in taking the initial steps that will lead to the eventual update and maintenance of the information. 
 
First Nations who have completed traditional use studies hold the detailed information regarding traditional 
use sites and values identified within their asserted traditional territories.  TUS information is not normally 
shared with forest licensees but where this information exists is considered by decision-makers when 
making statutory decisions.  
Approximately 230 proposed cutblocks (totalling roughly 6,300 ha) in TFL 6 have been intensively 
surveyed for CMTs.  This stand-level information has been entered into WFP‟s GIS database.  Of these, 
74 proposed cutblocks had CMTs found, and of those 74 cutblocks, 70 were within 1 kilometre of the 
ocean shoreline or the Marble River. 
To address the THLB impacts of protecting cultural heritage resources a 1% incremental netdown will be 
applied to the portion of TFL 6 that is both within an EFZ and within 1 km of the ocean; elsewhere it is 
assumed that management of cultural heritage resources is addressed by other netdowns (mainly stand-
level retention).  The 1% incremental netdown is an estimate of the incremental area retained specifically 
to conserve CMTs and other cultural heritage resources that is not addressed by other netdowns including 
stand-level retention discussed in the next section.

6.14 Area Reductions to Reflect Stand-level Retention in Cutblocks 
Stand-level retention is modelled based on two components: Wildlife Tree Retention Areas (WTRA – 
formerly referred to as Wildlife Tree Patches (WTPs)) and areas re tained to meet retention silviculture 
system requirements.  WTRA are associated with all cutblocks whereas the retention silviculture system is 
employed in varying amounts according to the Western Forest Strategy.  The stand-level retention 
netdowns are also assumed to partially or fully address management of cultural heritage resources and 
karst features (see Sections 6.13 and 6.16 respectivley). 
6.14.1 Wildlife Tree Retention Areas 
Where feasible and wildlife objectives can be met, WTRA are located in constrained areas such as 
riparian reserves, inoperable stands or unstable slopes.  In order to capture those WTRA located in 
otherwise harvestable areas a THLB area reduction is applied.   
The January 2005 order establishing OGMAs within the San Josef LU also established wildlife tree 
retention objectives by BEC subzone for that LU: 6% within CWHvh; 10% within CWHvm; 1% within 
MHmm.  For all other LUs it is assumed that the WTRA requirement is 7% for all BEC subzones.  The 
overall area-weighted WTRA requirement is 7.7%. 
6.14.2 Western Forest Strategy 
Western‟s Forest Strategy (WFS) includes a program for conserving biodiversity on company tenures.  
The approach is to vary the use of retention systems and the amount of stand level retention by Resource 
Management Zones of the Vancouver Island Land Use Plan and by ecosection and is being phased in 
over the next few years.  For this analysis it will be assumed the WFP strategy is fully implemented from 
the beginning of the analysis period. 
In Enhanced Forestry Zones (EFZs) the retention system will be used for 30% of the harvested area in the 
Nahwitti Lowland Ecosection and 50% of the harvested area in the Northern Island Mountains Ecosection 
found within TFL 6. The minimum long-term stand-level retention target is 10%.   
In General Management Zones (GMZs) the retention system will be used for 40% of the harvested area in 
the Nahwitti Lowland Ecosection and 60% of the harvested area in the Northern Island Mountains 
Ecosection found within TFL 6. The minimum long-term stand-level retention target is 15%.   
In Special Management Zones (SMZs) the VILUP Higher Level Plan Order specifies: “applying a variety of 
silvicultural systems, patch sizes and patch shapes across the zone, subject to a maximum cutblock size 
of 5 ha if clearcut, clearcut with reserves or seed tree silvicultural systems are applied, and 40 ha if 
shelterwood, selection or retention silvicultural systems are applied.”  A minimum of 20% long-term stand-
level retention is recommended for SMZs in the Western Forest Strategy based on both social and 
biological criteria. 

--- page 45 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 23 
This retention is long-term - it must remain for at least one rotation.  Applying the above retention system 
requirements to the Ecosection/VILUP Zone combinations present within TFL 6 results in at least 40% of 
the total harvest area being in retention system cutblocks (with the remaining being clearcut or clearcut-
with-reserves) and an area-weighted average overall minimum stand-level retention requirement of 
10.4%.  Assuming the first 7.7% of the stand-level retention target is for WTRA (see Section 6.14.1) and 
applies to all cutblocks, the minimum incremental impact of the WFP Forest Strategy is estimated to be an 
area-weighted average of 2.7% (10.4% minus 7.7%).   
6.14.3 THLB Netdown 
In MP#9, a uniform 4% volume reduction was applied to account for WTP retention and an analysis of the 
gross area of cutblocks harvested from 1997 to 2008 (12 years) indicated that was an appropriate 
netdown.  With the assumption for this timber supply analysis being that stand-level retention will be 
higher due to implementation of the Western Forest Strategy, an area-weighted average incremental 
netdown of 5% for stand-level retention will be used.  Rather than applying this netdown uniformly across 
the landbase, it is distributed by RMZ type, landscape unit and BEC subzone (see Table 16) to reflect the 
varying levels of WTRA and stand-level retention expected across these and as detailed above

6.16 Caves and Karst  
Portions of TFL 6 are underlain with limestone bedrock that forms part of the Quatsino formation.  As a 
result of the dissolving action of water on limestone (a soluble bedrock) karst topography (characterized 
by fluted and pitted rock surfaces, vertical shafts, sinkholes, sinking streams, springs, subsurface 
drainage systems, and caves) often forms.  On March 23, 2007, the District Manager of the NICCFD 
established a GAR order indentifying karst caves, important features and elements within very high and 
high vulnerability karst and significant surface karst features as resource features requiring management 
under FRPA.  
The impact of protecting karst features on timber supply is uncertain. To date, little area has been 
reserved during operational planning to protect karst features.  Estimates of impacts will improve as 
operational planning proceeds in karst areas.  For this analysis, no netdowns for karst management will be 
made as it is assumed that any reserves required are accounted for by the stand-level retention 
allowances (see Section 0). 
 

--- page 47 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 25 
7 INVENTORY AGGREGATION 
This section describes the delineation of the TFL landbase and definition of stand types needed to 
complete the timber supply analysis. The TFL area is categorized in a hierarchy of different management 
zones to allow for a variety of forest cover constraints (e.g. biodiversity).  Stand types are grouped in 
analysis units (AU) for projecting timber yields based on similar leading species, history and productivity.  
Areas within all tables in this section may not sum due to rounding to the nearest hectare. 
7.1 Management Zones 
Unique forest cover objectives will be modelled through the different management zones.  Landscape 
Units, Special Management Zones (SMZs), General Management Zones (GMZs) and Enhanced Forestry 
Zones (EFZs) are delineated in the data (Table 18 and Table 19) and will be used to apply forest cover 
constraints (see Section 10.2 for details).   
To reduce the number of unique resource management zones in the data, some minor revisions were 
made: 
1. The area within TFL 6 around the village of Port Alice that is not within a SMZ, GMZ or EFZ was 
assigned to the Mahatta-Neroutsos EFZ.  This affected 312 ha of productive forest and 189 ha of 
THLB. 
2. 11 ha of productive forest (10 ha of THLB) originally identified within the Nahwitti-Tsulquate GMZ 
were assigned to the Holberg EFZ.  The boundary between these two EFZ‟s is intended to be the 
TFL 6 boundary. 
3. Due to a different height-of-land interpretation, 110 ha of productive forest (46 ha of THLB) 
originally identified within the Klaskish GMZ were assigned to the Mahatta-Neroutsos EFZ. 
4. 31 ha of productive forest (6 ha of THLB) originally identified within the Marble River Protected 
Area were assigned to the Marble GMZ.  This revision is the result of different boundaries for the 
Marble River Park between the provincial park data and the RMZ data.

7.2 Analysis Units 
The forested area in the THLB is aggregated into groups of similar stands to produce growth and yield 
information needed to model timber supply. For existing stands, analysis units (AUs) are based on 
ecosystem groupings within site productivity classes, age class, leading species, and silviculture 
treatments.  For future stands the leading species criteria is replaced with establishment method (planting 
or natural regeneration).  These criteria are described in more detail in the following sections. 
7.2.1 Ecosystem assignment 
Ecosystems were assigned using the TFL 6 Ecosite classification system completed in 1985 by Terence 
Lewis, Ph.D., P.Ag., P.Geo..  This classification system differs from the later published MoFR‟s BEC 
system (1994) in that it also incorporates terrain types.  In 2007, Lewis updated the classification system 
based on learnings and research results from the previous 20 years.  The revised classification system 
aligns more closely to the MoFR‟s BEC system.  The 1985 Lewis classification system is being used to 
define analysis units in this analysis due to site productivity estimates being based on that system (see 
section 8.1 for details).  Each polygon in the TFL was assigned to one analysis unit level ecosite.  To limit 
the number of unique combinations some ecosites were grouped based on similar site productivity and 
similar future management assumptions (Table 20).

7.2.2 Age class 
Stands were assigned to five different age classes based on management era.  Ages are based on known 
or estimated ages as of the year 2011.  2011 was chosen as this is the mid-year of the first five-year 
period (2009-2013) of the analysis so this represents the average age, and therefore average estimated 
volume, for the first period of the analysis. 
 

--- page 52 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 30 
7.2.2.1 Unmanaged Stands 
7.2.2.1.1 Mature 
Mature stands are defined as stands greater than 140 years of age.  Volume in these stands is estimated 
using MFML‟s Variable Density Yield Projection (VDYP) version 6.6d. 
7.2.2.1.2 Immature 
These are stands that are between 51 years old and 140 years old.  The assumption is that these stands 
are the results of natural regeneration following harvesting or natural disturbances. Volume in these 
stands is estimated using VDYP. 
7.2.2.2 Managed Stands 
These stands have been established since active forest management began within the TFL.  Most of 
these stands are the result of planting but there are naturally regenerated stands present in these age 
ranges.  Volume in these stands is estimated using MFML‟s Table Interpolation Program for Stand Yields 
(TIPSY) version 4.1. 
7.2.2.2.1 Stands aged 11 – 50 years 
These stands have been established since the inception of active forest management but with insignificant 
genetic gain values.  Natural regeneration is more prevalent in this age range (especially the older ages in 
this range) than in the 1-10 year old range. 
7.2.2.2.2 Stands aged 1 – 10 years 
These most recently established stands have genetic gain values and, on average, higher establishment 
densities than the older managed stands.  The higher establishment densities are intended to reflect the 
higher proportion of planting compared to the older managed stands. 
7.2.2.2.3 Future stands 
These stands have genetic gain values equal to the 1 – 10 year old stands and will assume to be 
influenced by higher levels of stand-level retention from the previous harvest due to the use of the 
retention silviculture system. 
7.2.3 Leading Species 
For existing stands, the leading species is used to differentiate analysis units in order to better reflect the 
differences in expected growth rates by species.  To reduce the possible number of unique combinations, 
some resulting analysis units are combined where this differentiation resulted in little area (generally less 
than 5 ha) within an analysis unit. 

--- page 53 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 31 
7.2.4 Silviculture Treatments 
For existing stands, fertilization and juvenile spacing treatments are used to differentiate analysis units in 
order to better reflect the differences in expected growth rates in stands following these treatments.  For 
analysis unit allocation, fertilization takes precedence over spacing if a stand has been both fertilized and 
spaced.  Again, to reduce the number of unique combinations, consequential analysis units are combined 
where this differentiation resulted in little area (usually less than 5 ha) within an analysis unit.  For future 
stands, fertilization of a proportion of S1CH ecosites (cedar-salal or “SCHIRP” sites) will be modelled (see 
section 8.6.2 for details) and it is assumed no juvenile spacing will take place. 
7.2.5 Regeneration Method 
Natural regeneration of western hemlock and balsam can be relied upon to meet regeneration 
requirements on some sites within TFL 6: typically the S1HA, S2, M1, M2 and M4 ecosites.  Natural 
regeneration success has been more prevalent in the former Jeune Landing operation than elsewhere.  
For future stands, natural regeneration will be modelled on a proportion of the appropriate ecosites with 
the rest being modelled assuming regeneration by planting (see section 8.6.1 for details). 
7.2.6 Analysis unit codes 
A 4-digit code identifies the ecosite group, age class, leading species (for existing stands) or regeneration 
method (for future stands) and silviculture treatment for each analysis unit (Table 21 and Table 22).

8 GROWTH AND YIELD 
This section describes the approach used to develop yield tables for managed and natural stands. The 
general approach is to develop yield tables for existing and future stands, thus specific yield tables are 
developed for: 
1) Existing natural mature stands. 
2) Existing natural immature stands. 
3) Existing managed stands. 
4) Future managed stands. 
8.1 Site Index 
Site index (the total height of the dominant trees in a stand at age 50 years) is the most common measure 
of forest site productivity and forest growth used in British Columbia.  It provides a numeric description of 
site productivity that enables forest managers to predict forest stand growth and the yield of timber at 
harvest.  Site index allows the comparison of productive potential between sites across a broad range of 
existing stand conditions. 
Site indices for the main commercial tree species are the same as used in MP #9 and are based on a 
study completed by J.S. Thrower and Associates (now part of Teco Resource Management Group) in 
1997 (see Appendix A).  This study determined statistically-based average site indices by tree species and 
productivity groups created by grouping Lewis ecological units having similar site potential.  
Table 23 shows the site index by species and productivity group and the THLB area-weighted average by 
species. 
Table 23 - Site Index Values by Species and Productivity Group

8.2 Utilization Levels 
The utilization level is 12.5 cm DBH for all existing managed stands (stands less than 51 years old) and 
for future stands.  Stump height for these stands is 30 cm and top diameter inside bark (DIB) is 10 cm.  
Utilization level for natural stands is 17.5 cm DBH, with stump height of 30 cm and top DIB of 15 cm 

--- page 56 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 34 
(Table 24).  Operationally, natural immature stands (age 51 -140 years) are utilized to a 12.5 cm DBH; 
however, volumes for these stands are being estimated using VDYP and VDYP does not accept the 
smaller DBH standards for TFL 6.

8.3 Operational Adjustment Factors 
Managed stand yield tables are created using TIPSY.  Unadjusted TIPSY output reflects the growth 
relationships observed in research plots that were generally located in fully-stocked, even-aged stands of 
uniform site and in forests of little or no pest activity.  To reflect operational environments, two operational 
adjustment factors (OAFs) are applied to TIPSY outputs to reduce the potential yields.  OAF 1 is constant 
across all ages and is intended to account for small, unmapped non-productive areas in a stand and 
competition from non-commercial tree species and brush.  OAF 2 increases with age and is intended to 
reflect the impact of insects, disease and decay.  Typically OAF 1 is 15%; however, results from random 
samples placed in second growth stands within the TFL indicate a low proportion of unmapped non-
productive area such that an OAF 1 of 10% (as was used in MP #9) better reflects the stocking levels 
achieved on TFL 6. 
Adjustments to managed stand volumes are the same as those used in MP #9: 
1. OAF 1: 10% 
2. OAF 2: 5% 
8.4 Volume Reductions 
8.4.1 Unmanaged Stands Volume 
Unmanaged stands volumes are estimated using VDYP.  VDYP normally computes the volume of 
standing wood less the amount that has decayed.  Default VDYP waste and breakage values for TFL 6 
will be applied to account for wood lost due to falling and yarding (breakage) and wood lost due to short 
log segments and other reasons (waste) plus the sound wood portion of a tree or log which has more than 
50% decay (waste2). 
8.4.2 Managed Stands Volume 
With the implementation of the Western Forest Strategy for conserving biological diversity (see section 
6.14.2 for details) future stands volumes will be reduced to account for the growth impact of stand-level 
retention from the previous harvest.  A volume reduction of 2% will be applied to all future stands in 
Enhanced Forestry Zones where stand-level retention is assumed to average at least 10%; a 3% volume 

--- page 57 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 35 
reduction will be applied to all future stands in General Management Zones where average stand-level 
retention will be at least 15%; and a 5% volume reduction will be applied to all future stands in Special 
Management Zones to account for stand-level retention of at least 20%.  These reductions are based on 
similar assumptions used in previous analyses for TFLs 39 and 44.  The general notion that growth 
impacts on forest regeneration increase with greater retention has been observed in early results from 
research being conducted by Dr. Nick Smith within TFLs 39 and 44.  
The yield reduction will occur when individual stands are harvested during modelling.  Yield curves are left 
unaltered.  
8.5 Yield Tables for Unmanaged Stands  
Unmanaged stands are those stands that are projected to be greater than 50 years old in the year 2011.  
The projected ages of these stands are based on the adjusted ages in the VRI database for 2001, which 
was the reference year for the statistical adjustment (see Appendix B).  Adjusted site indices were used as 
heights in 2011 are not known.  Finally the volume adjustment factors were applied.  Crown closure values 
from the inventory were used except for those stands that were less than 50 years old in 1995 (the year of 
the photos used in Phase I of the VRI process and therefore the year of the crown closure estimate).  In 
VDYP, stands younger than 50 years old use default crown closure values by species. 
VDYP outputs were generated for every applicable resultant polygon and then area-weighted averages 
were determined for each analysis unit.  Separate tables were generated for the THLB and the non-
contributing landbase – only the THLB yields are included in this document.  
8.5.1 Existing Mature Stand Volumes 
The timber volume in existing mature stands (those > 140 years old in 2011) was determined for each 
analysis unit by calculating the area-weighted average inventory volumes and species composition (Table 
25).  The yield curves for each mature analysis unit are listed and shown in Appendix C.

8.6 Yield Tables for Managed Stands 
8.6.1 Stocking density 
 
A significant planting program has existed in TFL 6 since the early 1960‟s.  For the last 15 to 20 years 
most of the harvested area has been planted, typically at planting levels of around 1,200 sph and many 
areas also include substantial natural in-growth.  TIPSY does not directly model planted stands with 
natural in-growth.  Planted stands currently aged 1 to 10 years and future stands are modelled at initial 
densities such that the effect of the OAFs reduces stand densities to approximately the target densities at 
free-growing ages (generally 15 years).  Initial densities for naturally regenerated stands reflect a 
generalization of densities found at regeneration delay and free-growing dates. 
Stands currently aged 11 to 50 years are modelled with lower initial densities (relative to younger current 
and future stands) due to a higher reliance on natural regeneration. 
8.6.2 Fertilization 
Since 1986, nitrogen fertilization (post-establishment) has occurred on approximately 15,000 ha in TFL 6, 
with approximately 4,500 ha having received two treatments.  The fertilization treatments have mostly 
occurred within the S1CH and S1HA ecosites.  The treatments have typically been mid-late rotation of 
Douglas fir with urea (N200) or urea and phosphorous (N200/P75) applied at free-growing plus urea only 

--- page 60 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 38 
about 10 years later on the cedar sites (S1CH).  The fertilization program has been contingent on 
government funding programs and is expected to continue in the next few years. 
Fertilization is incorporated into the yield tables for current and future stands.  The impact of fertilization is 
modelled for cedar, hemlock and spruce by using the site index from the next higher productivity group.  
The default TIPSY increases are used for Douglas fir with the assumption being that a single treatment is 
done at age 30 years.  No volume gains are modelled for any other species.  The gains for cedar, 
hemlock and spruce are supported by the most recent (fall of 2009) measurements (not yet published 
Master degree thesis) at the „SCHIRP‟ (Salal Cedar Hemlock Integrated Research Program) site located 
within TFL 6 near Port McNeill. 
8.6.3 Volumes for Existing Managed Stands Aged 11 - 50 Years 
Silviculture assumptions for existing managed stands aged 11 – 50 years included a plantation 
regeneration method for all stands, species composition from the inventory database, establishment 
density based on inventory and free-growing stand data and expected relative stocking success.  These 
silviculture assumptions and site index estimates by species were used as input into Batch TIPSY 4.1 with 
outputs generated for every applicable resultant polygon and then area-weighted averages determined for 
each analysis unit.  See Table 27 for the average of the inputs by analysis unit.   No genetic gain was 
applied to stands in this age range.

8.6.4 Volumes for Existing Managed Stands Aged 1 - 10 Years 
Silviculture assumptions for existing managed stands aged 1 – 10 years included a plantation 
regeneration method for all stands, species composition from the inventory database (simplified to 
eliminate minor species present) or future stand species assumptions where no current species 
information was available, and establishment density that reflects stocking success.  These silviculture 
assumptions and site index estimates by species were used as input into Batch TIPSY 4.1 with outputs 
generated for every applicable resultant polygon and then area-weighted averages determined for each 
analysis unit.  Genetic gain for Cw, Cy, Fd and Hw was applied to stands in this age range based on 
average values for the most common seedlots planted in TFL 6 since 2000. Expected GW values for Hw 
are reduced to reflect a component of natural regeneration expected in the harvested stands.

8.6.5 Regeneration Delay 
The regeneration delay refers to the average time between harvesting and establishment of the next 
rotation.  The average regeneration delay as reported in TFL 6 Annual Sustainable Forest Management 
Plan Indicator Reports through until 2008 has decreased until it is now approximately 0.8 years.   
The majority of harvested area is planted, with approximately 20% of harvested area being regenerated 
naturally.  Planted seedlings are typically one year old.  Hence the regeneration delay from harvest until 
germination of the next crop of planted trees is generally less than 1 year.  Also, early seedling growth is 
assisted on some sites (e.g. cedar-salal sites) in TFL 6 by the practice of fertilization at time of planting.  
A one year regeneration delay for planted future managed stands is incorporated into the yield tables that 
will be used in the analyses.  For naturally regenerated stands a 3-year regeneration delay is incorporated 
into the corresponding yield tables.

regeneration component in future harvested stands.

8.6.6 Future Stand Volumes 
Ecologically-based silviculture strategies for future stands were developed by Western Forest Products 
staff based on current practice (Table 29).  Initial densities were chosen such that the effect of the OAFs 
reduces stand densities to approximately the target densities at free-growing ages (generally 15 years) 
and to reflect both the continued practice to plant almost all harvested areas and the natural in-growth 
experienced on many sites.  Species and stocking levels are portrayed at this broad average level to 
simplify modelling.  It is recognized that this includes a range of specific prescriptions that for example 
might include establishment of alder on a small % of the landbase (for further discussion on this see 
Hardwood Management in the Coast Forest Region (MoFR, 2009)). 
Projections of average Genetic Worth (GW) were developed from WFP‟s Saanich Forestry Centre seed  
inventory.  Expected GW values for Hw are reduced to reflect a component of natural regeneration 
expected in the harvested stands.  Average values for GW by species and analysis unit listed in Table 29 
will be applied to future managed stands.   
See Appendix G for the yield tables.  In the timber supply model, future stands yields will be reduced to 
account for the impact on growth of the next crop by trees retained to meet stand-level retention 
requirements (see Section 8.4.2 for details).

9 NON-RECOVERABLE LOSSES 
Windthrow, insects, disease and fire can cause catastrophic losses of whole stands of trees.  Over the 
long-term the probability of losses to such natural causes can be predicted.  Where losses occur in 
merchantable stands some of the dead or dying timber may be salvageable.  When modelling timber 
supply, unsalvaged losses are added to the desired harvest forecast and then subtracted from the 
forecast upon completion of the modelling exercise. 
The analysis will model 7,000 m3/year of non-recoverable losses. 
9.1 Windthrow 
Historically, windthrow has occurred mainly in relatively small patches.  Loss of single trees or small 
groups are mostly accounted for in inventory sampling for existing timber yield estimates and OAFs 
applied for young stands.  A great deal of research has been done over the past 10 years to try to 
determine the variables that affect the amount of expected windthrow along cutblock edges following 
harvest and the effectiveness of various edge treatment techniques (e.g. pruning, topping, feathering) to 
reduce the amount of windthrow experienced.  The results of this research aid in cutblock design and 
treatment prescriptions such that the amount of windthrow experienced due to endemic winds is greatly 
reduced.   
Operational staff estimates for unsalvaged losses due to windthrow are in the range of 5 – 10 hectares per 
year.  If the upper end of this range is assumed and an average volume of 700 m3/ha is used, the resulting 
losses are approximately 7,000 m3/year.  In MP #9 non-recoverable losses from windthrow were 
estimated at 7,000 m3/year.  The landbase for MP #10 is smaller due to removals from the TFL (see 
section 6.1) and the AAC is expected to decrease; therefore the amount of non-recoverable losses would 
logically decrease.  However, counteracting those factors is increased stand-level retention due to 
Western‟s Forest Strategy (see section 6.14.2) that will create more patches and therefore edges of 
timber that will be subjected to winds.  Therefore, an estimate of 7,000 m3/year for non-recoverable losses 
will be used again in this analysis. 
9.2 Insects and Disease 
The forests of TFL 6 have been relatively free of major insect or disease infestations and therefore no 
losses are associated.  There have been no major catastrophic outbreaks causing significant unsalvaged 
mortality or volume losses.  The main active agent has been Spruce weevil (Pissodes strobe) which has 
infested Sitka spruce plantations to the point where the species is a minor component in reforestation 
programs.  WFP has a weevil resistant orchard producing seedlings with an average 86% resistance 
(meaning that on average 7% of the progeny from this orchard are projected to display attack in a given 
year).  These highly resistant seedlings are used wherever spruce is a suitable species and site conditions 
indicate a high hazard of weevil infestation.  Between 2003 and 2008 approximately 160,000 spruce 
seedlings were planted in high hazard cutblocks – all weevil resistant seedlings.  The last notable outbreak 
by other insect species was in the late 1980‟s when an outbreak of black-headed budworm (Acleris 

--- page 66 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 44 
gloverana) was detected on approximately 7,000 ha, mainly in the vicinity of the head of Holberg Inlet.  
Spray trials of Bacillis thuringiensis (B.T.) were conducted in 1989 and 1990 with good success.  By 1991 
the population had collapsed and in most cases the damage done appeared to be temporary as new 
foliage was evident. 
Hemlock dwarf mistletoe is widespread throughout merchantable sized stands.  Sanitation treatments of 
advanced regeneration are sometimes required to prevent the spread in newly regenerated western 
hemlock stands.  Usually regenerated stands are not impacted significantly by hemlock dwarf mistletoe.   
Root diseases sometimes result in small pockets of mortality.  These losses are assumed accounted for 
by the operational adjustment factors (OAFs) applied to yield curves.  Impacts of root disease (Armillaria 
ostoyae) and laminated root rot (Phellinus weirii) are less than observed in other areas.  Additional OAF 
allowances are not applied. 
9.3 Fire 
The risk of loss of timber due to fire is relatively low within the TFL.  The bulk of the TFL has a wet climate 
characterized by cool, wet summers and fire suppression has been efficient; hence the likelihood of losses 
to forest fire is small. 

--- page 67 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 45

10.2  Forest Cover Requirements  
10.2.1 Visual Quality 
The Visual Landscape Inventory (VLI) for TFL 6 was updated between 2003 and 2005.  This updated 
inventory was accepted by the District Manager (DM) of the NICCFD in June 2010 and was used as the 
basis for undertaking the required process for establishing legal visual quality objectives (VQOs) within 
scenic areas under GAR.  The proposed VQOs were incorporated into the data set. 

--- page 68 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 46 
On September 24, 2010, the DM signed the Order to Establish Visual Quality Objectives for Tree Farm 
Licence 6 and Block 7 of the Pacific TSA within the North Island Central Coast Forest District.  The 
established VQO classes and VQO polygons differ slightly from the proposed VQOs due to comments 
received during the review process. The established VQO classes will be used in the analysis; however 
the polygon revisions will not be incorporated (as this requires the entire data set to be recreated).  The 
minor polygon differences result in 97 ha of THLB that fall within an established VQO polygon not being 
modelled. 
VQOs within scenic areas to be modelled in the timber supply analysis are Retention (R), Partial Retention 
(PR) and Modification (M).  The amount of area that can be disturbed (i.e. has not achieved visually 
effective green-up) is 5%, 15% and 25% for each VQO respectively.  These levels are set at the upper 
end of the % disturbance range for use in timber supply analyses as visual landscape design during 
cutblock layout has become common practice in sensitive viewscapes.  Cutblock designs that follow the 
lines and forms of the viewscape allow more timber to be removed and still meet the VQO when 
compared to unnatural cutblock shapes.  Additionally, the use of the retention silviculture system can 
result in more timber removal in visually sensitive areas by strategically placing retention patches to act as 
visual screens.  A sensitivity analysis in which these percentages are reduced to the mid-point of the 
range for each VQO (2.5%, 10% and 20% respectively) will indicate the sensitivity of timber supply to 
management of visual quality objectives and the design of cutblocks within visually sensitive areas.  
A 5 m visually effective green-up (VEG) height is proposed for TFL 6.  As Woodstock uses volume over 
age curves for yield tables, an age surrogate of 15 years old (3 periods) will be established to represent 
VEG height for each analysis unit.  
Table 31 outlines the management assumptions for dealing with visual quality within the TFL.

10.2.2 Adjacent Cutblock Green-up 
A 3 metre green-up height in General and Special Management Zones will be used for areas without 
established visual quality objectives.  A 10 year (2 periods) age surrogate will be used within the model to 
represent the 3 m height. This is a reasonable approximation (on average) with prompt planting, planting 
of improved seedlings and fertilization at time of planting in some areas.  As the Woodstock model applies 
cover class constraints by period and the analysis will use five-year periods it is believed ten years is more 
appropriate than fifteen years for the 3m requirement. A 1.3 metre green-up height in Enhanced Forestry 
Zones will be used for areas without established visual quality objectives.  A 5 year (1 period) age 
surrogate will be used within the model to represent the 1.3 m height.  Since Woodstock does not have 
the capability to spatially model adjacency requirements, a proxy will be used: a maximum of 25% of the 
THLB within a zone but outside of VQO polygons will be permitted to be less than the green-up age (5 or 
10 years depending on the zone type). 

--- page 69 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 47 
10.2.3 Landscape Level Biodiversity 
Landscape Units and Biodiversity Emphasis Options (BEOs) were designated through the Order 
Establishing Provincial Non-Spatial Old Growth Objectives effective June 30, 2004 (NSOG order).  This 
order is in effect until Old Growth Management Areas (OGMAs) are spatially determined through 
Landscape Unit planning.  For TFL 6 OGMAs have been established within the San Josef (Intermediate 
BEO) landscape unit (January 2005) and the Marble (Intermediate BEO) landscape unit (July 2010).  Draft 
OGMAs in the Holberg (Low BEO), Keogh (Low BEO), and Neroutsos (Low BEO) landscape units have 
been identified to meet the NSOG order (refer to Table 11).   
For the Mahattta (Low BEO) landscape unit draft OGMAs have been identified for the WFP tenure (TFL 6 
and unregulated Timber Licences) portion of the landscape unit only.  These draft OGMAs meet the old 
seral target within TFL 6 drawn down to 1/3 for the first rotation  
These draft OGMAs will be used in the timber supply analysis but must complete a public and First 
Nations‟ review process before coming legal and may be revised prior to that review process. 
For the forest types within TFL 6, old forest is defined as stands >250 years old.  The old seral target is 
based on the combination of BEO, BEC variant, and the natural disturbance type (NDT) of the variant.  
The draft OGMAs for landscape units with a Low BEO identify enough area to meet the old seral target 
drawn down to 1/3 for the first rotation (80 years).  Where the draft OGMAs do not meet the full target an 
old seral stage cover constraint will be applied.  The constraint for the end of the second rotation (160 
years) will be 2/3 of the full target, with the full old seral target being achieved by the end of the third 
rotation (240 years).  Table 32 indicates the landscape biodiversity constraints that will be applied for old 
seral forest.  For a breakdown of the current forest age by landscape unit and variant see Table 19.

10.2.4 Community Watersheds 
There is one designated community watershed (CWS) within TFL 6 - Calbick Creek (930.003) is a 64 ha 
CWS (with 55 ha of THLB) located between Quatse Lake and Coal Harbour.  Even though this CWS is no 
longer being used as a source for drinking water by the Quatsino First Nation (the water licence holder), a 
forest cover constraint will be applied so no more than 10% of the productive area within the watershed 
will be covered with stands less than 10 years old. 
10.2.5 Fisheries Sensitive Watersheds 
There are no established Fisheries Sensitive Watersheds (FSWs) within TFL 6.   
10.2.6 Rate-of-harvest Restrictions 
The FSP for TFL 6 contains a strategy for addressing Objective 8 of the VILUP HLP order (mitigate 
adverse hydrological impacts of practices permitted in EFZ‟s).  The FSP strategy includes preparing a 
watershed assessment and to the extent practicable, implementing the recommendations in such an 
assessment.  An assessment was completed in 2007 from which a set of watershed management 
strategies were developed (October 2007).  
The intent of the watershed management strategies is to allow for continued recovery in watersheds 
impacted by past practices, particularly by reducing the incidence of landslides.  From a timber supply 
analysis perspective, the strategies include limiting the rate-of-harvest on “steep terrain” in four 
watersheds: Colonial, Goodspeed, Hathaway and Koprino.  “Steep terrain” is defined as areas classified in 
a terrain stability inventory as Class IV (potentially unstable) or Class V (unstable) plus areas where the 
slope gradient is greater than 60%.   
No specific harvest rate limits were recommended in the original management strategies document.  Later 
follow-up with the professional who conducted the watershed assessment and developed the watershed 
management strategies resulted in a recommendation of limiting harvest on steep terrain in the Colonial 
watershed to approximately 135 ha over a 10 year period.  The 135 ha equates to roughly 1/6 of the 
“steep” THLB estimated to be within the Colonial watershed for this analysis. 
As the frequency of past landslides in the other three watersheds is similar to that in the Colonial 
watershed, the same ratio (1/6) will be used for the rate-of-harvest limits for steep terrain in the 
Goodspeed, Hathaway and Koprino watersheds (Table 33).

    February 2011

Higher Level Plans 
The order establishing Resource Management Zones and Resource Management Zone objectives within 
the area covered by the Vancouver Island Land Use Plan came into effect as of December 1, 2000.  Each 
Special Management Zone (SMZ) established by the order has an objective of maintaining mature seral 
forest over one quarter to one third of the forested area of the SMZ. 
There are portions of two SMZ‟s within TFL 6 – SMZ 2 (West Coast Nahwitti Lowlands) and SMZ 4 
(Koprino).  For this analysis, a constraint will be incorporated that maintains 25% of the productive forest 
land base in the mature and/or old seral stage within these SMZ‟s. 
10.3 Timber Harvesting 
10.3.1 Minimum Harvestable Age 
Minimum harvestable ages are simply minimum criteria for use in the timber supply model.  While actual 
harvesting may occur in stands at or below the minimum requirements, in order to meet forest-level 
objectives (i.e. maintaining overall timber flows), other stands will not be harvested until well past the 
minimum ages because consideration of other resource values may take precedence or timber may be in 
ample supply.   
In the previous two analyses minimum harvestable ages were selected as the age closest to the time 
when average stand diameter-at-breast-height (DBH) reached a threshold diameter that varied by site 
productivity class (see Table 34).   This criteria was selected on the basis of values and cost structures 
associated with current harvesting and manufacturing systems. 

--- page 72 ---
    February 2011 
 
TFL 6 – Timber Supply Analysis Information Package Page 50 
 
Table 34 – Minimum harvest criteria used in past two analyses 
Site Productivity Class 
Minimum Average

This analysis will again use minimum harvest ages based on average stand diameters; however, this time 
the average diameters will vary by harvesting system rather than site productivity - the notion being that 
larger diameters in general reflect higher values.  To have an economically sustainable harvesting 
program, average stand values must be greater than average harvesting costs.  Average harvesting costs 
are lowest for ground-based systems (e.g. skidder, “hoe-chucking”) and are highest for helicopter, with 
cable systems (e.g. grapple yarding) costs falling between those.   
The data set prepared for the analysis includes logging system (ground, cable or heli) based on the 
combination of operability class (see section 6.6) and slope class.  Conventionally operable areas with a 
slope between 0% and 30% are assumed harvestable by ground-based systems and all conventionally 
operable areas on steeper slopes are assumed harvestable by cable systems.  Helicopter operable areas 
are found across all slope classes.  Table 35 indicates the minimum stand-average DBH that will be used 
in the analysis and the resulting range of ages.  The younger ages are on higher productivity sites while 
the older ages are on lower productivity sites. 
 
Table 35 - Minimum Harvest Criteria

Ground 30 cm 40 – 115 70.4
